<ol style="list-style-type: lower-alpha; margin-left: 1em; font-size:85%;">
<li value="1"><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_a"><b><a href="#ref_a">^a</a></b></span> The area of Poland, as given by the Central Statistical Office, is 312,679&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (120,726&#160;sq&#160;mi), of which 311,888&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (120,421&#160;sq&#160;mi) is land and 791&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (305&#160;sq&#160;mi) is internal water surface area.<sup id="cite_ref-CSO_2008_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CSO_2008-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
<li value="2"><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_b"><b><a href="#ref_b">^b</a></b></span> The adoption of Christianity in Poland is seen by many Poles, regardless of their religious affiliation or lack thereof, as one of the most significant events in their country's history, as it was used to unify the tribes in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></li>
</ol>

<p><b>Poland</b> (<a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>: <span lang="pl"><i>Polska</i></span> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Polish" title="Help:IPA for Polish">[ˈpɔlska]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata">&#160;(<a href="/wiki/File:Pl-Polska.ogg" title="File:Pl-Polska.ogg"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/13px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png" width="13" height="13" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/20px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/26px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="500" /></a> <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/98/Pl-Polska.ogg" class="internal" title="Pl-Polska.ogg">listen</a>)</small>), officially the <b>Republic of Poland</b> (Polish: <span lang="pl"><i><a href="/wiki/Rzeczpospolita" title="Rzeczpospolita">Rzeczpospolita</a> Polska</i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="ref_anone"><a href="#endnote_anone">[a]</a></sup> <span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Pl-Rzeczpospolita_Polska.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/56/Pl-Rzeczpospolita_Polska.ogg" class="internal" title="Pl-Rzeczpospolita Polska.ogg">listen</a></span>&#160;<small class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">(<a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help"><span style="cursor:help;">help</span></a>·<a href="/wiki/File:Pl-Rzeczpospolita_Polska.ogg" title="File:Pl-Rzeczpospolita Polska.ogg"><span style="cursor:help;">info</span></a>)</small></span>), is a country in <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Fawn2013_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fawn2013-10">[10]</a></sup> situated between the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> in the north and two <a href="/wiki/Mountain_range" title="Mountain range">mountain ranges</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Sudetes" title="Sudetes">Sudetes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Mountains" title="Carpathian Mountains">Carpathian Mountains</a>) in the south. <a href="/wiki/Borders_of_Poland" title="Borders of Poland">Bordered</a> by Germany to the west; the Czech Republic and Slovakia to the south; Ukraine and Belarus to the east; and the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kaliningrad_Oblast" title="Kaliningrad Oblast">Kaliningrad Oblast</a> (a Russian <a href="/wiki/Enclave_and_exclave" title="Enclave and exclave">exclave</a>) and Lithuania to the north. The total <a href="/wiki/Area_of_Poland" title="Area of Poland">area of Poland</a> is 312,679 square kilometres (120,726&#160;sq&#160;mi),<sup id="cite_ref-CSO_2008_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CSO_2008-8">[8]</a></sup> making it the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_area" class="mw-redirect" title="List of countries and dependencies by area">69th largest country</a> in the world and the 9th largest in Europe. With a population of over 38.5&#160;million people,<sup id="cite_ref-CSO_2008_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CSO_2008-8">[8]</a></sup> Poland is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_population" class="mw-redirect" title="List of countries by population">34th most populous country</a> in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-nationmaster_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nationmaster-11">[11]</a></sup> the 8th most populous country in Europe and the sixth most populous <a href="/wiki/Member_state_of_the_European_Union" title="Member state of the European Union">member of the European Union</a>. Poland is a <a href="/wiki/Unitary_state" title="Unitary state">unitary state</a> divided into 16 <a href="/wiki/Voivodeships_of_Poland" title="Voivodeships of Poland">administrative subdivisions</a>, and its capital and largest <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_and_towns_in_Poland" title="List of cities and towns in Poland">city</a> is <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>. Other metropolises include <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wroc%C5%82aw" title="Wrocław">Wrocław</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pozna%C5%84" title="Poznań">Poznań</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gda%C5%84sk" title="Gdańsk">Gdańsk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Szczecin" title="Szczecin">Szczecin</a>.</p>
<p>The establishment of a Polish state can be traced back to 966, when <a href="/wiki/Mieszko_I_of_Poland" title="Mieszko I of Poland">Mieszko I</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-A_Concise_History_of_Poland_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Concise_History_of_Poland-12">[12]</a></sup> ruler of a territory roughly coextensive with that of present-day Poland, converted to Christianity. The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1025%E2%80%931385)" title="Kingdom of Poland (1025–1385)">Kingdom of Poland</a> was founded in 1025, and in 1569 it cemented <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_union" title="Polish–Lithuanian union">a longstanding political association</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Grand Duchy of Lithuania</a> by signing the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_Lublin" title="Union of Lublin">Union of Lublin</a>. This union formed the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a>, one of the largest and most populous countries of 16th and 17th century Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wandycz2001-66_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wandycz2001-66-14">[14]</a></sup> Once a leading European power with a <a href="/wiki/Golden_Liberty" title="Golden Liberty">uniquely progressive political system</a>, the Commonwealth ceased to exist as an independent state, following several <a href="/wiki/Partitions_of_Poland" title="Partitions of Poland">territorial partitions</a> among <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia" title="Kingdom of Prussia">Prussia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Monarchy" title="Habsburg Monarchy">Austria</a> from 1772 to 1795. <a href="/wiki/History_of_Poland_(1918%E2%80%9339)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Poland (1918–39)">Poland regained its independence</a> in 1918 at the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polish-Soviet_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish-Soviet war">reconstituting much of its historical territory</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Second_Polish_Republic" title="Second Polish Republic">Second Polish Republic</a>.</p>
<p>In September 1939, <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> started with the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland" title="Invasion of Poland">invasion of Poland</a> by <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>, followed shortly thereafter by <a href="/wiki/Soviet_invasion_of_Poland" title="Soviet invasion of Poland">invasion</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, both in accordance with the <a href="/wiki/Molotov%E2%80%93Ribbentrop_Pact" title="Molotov–Ribbentrop Pact">Molotov–Ribbentrop Pact</a>. More than six million Polish citizens died in the war.<sup id="cite_ref-posterum0_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-posterum0-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-szma_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-szma-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aftermath_of_World_War_II" title="Aftermath of World War II">After the war</a>, Poland's borders were <a href="/wiki/Territorial_changes_of_Poland_immediately_after_World_War_II" title="Territorial changes of Poland immediately after World War II">shifted westwards</a> under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_Conference#Poland" title="Potsdam Conference">Potsdam Conference</a>. With the backing of the Soviet Union, a communist <a href="/wiki/Polish_Committee_of_National_Liberation" title="Polish Committee of National Liberation">puppet government</a> was formed, and after a falsified <a href="/wiki/Polish_people%27s_referendum,_1946" title="Polish people's referendum, 1946">referendum</a> in 1946, the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Poland" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Republic of Poland">People's Republic of Poland</a> was established as a Soviet <a href="/wiki/Satellite_state" title="Satellite state">satellite state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1989" title="Revolutions of 1989">Revolutions of 1989</a> Poland's Communist government was overthrown and Poland adopted a new constitution establishing itself as a democracy, informally called the <b>Third Polish Republic</b>.</p>
<p>Despite <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_casualties_of_Poland" title="World War II casualties of Poland">the large number of casualties</a> and destruction the country <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Poland_(1939%E2%80%9345)" class="mw-redirect" title="Occupation of Poland (1939–45)">experienced during World War II</a>, Poland has managed to preserve much of <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Poland" title="Culture of Poland">its rich cultural wealth</a>, including 14 <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_World_Heritage_Sites_of_Poland" title="List of World Heritage Sites of Poland">World Heritage Sites</a><sup id="cite_ref-Poland_.E2.80.93_UNESCO_World_Heritage_Centre_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poland_.E2.80.93_UNESCO_World_Heritage_Centre-18">[18]</a></sup> and 54 <a href="/wiki/List_of_Historic_Monuments_(Poland)" title="List of Historic Monuments (Poland)">Historical Monuments</a>, among many other <a href="/wiki/Objects_of_cultural_heritage_in_Poland" title="Objects of cultural heritage in Poland">objects of cultural heritage</a>.</p>
<p>Since the early 1990s, when the transition to a primarily market-based economy began, Poland has achieved a "very high" ranking on the <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Human_Development_Index_and_its_components_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Development_Index_and_its_components-19">[19]</a></sup> as well as improving economic freedom.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Poland is a democratic country, which was categorised by the World Bank as having a <a href="/wiki/World_Bank_high-income_economy" title="World Bank high-income economy">high-income economy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-worldbank8_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worldbank8-21">[21]</a></sup> and is ranked in the top 30 for standard of living.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Furthermore, it is visited by approximately 16 million tourists every year (2014), which makes it the 16th most visited country in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Poland is the eighth largest <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_European_Union" title="Economy of the European Union">economy in the European Union</a><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and was the 6th fastest growing economy on the continent between 2010 and 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Global_Peace_Index" title="Global Peace Index">Global Peace Index</a> for 2014, Poland is ranked 19th in the list of the safest countries in the world to live in.<sup id="cite_ref-SzefsBielawska_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SzefsBielawska-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Name_of_Poland" title="Name of Poland">Name of Poland</a></div>
<p>The origin of the name <i>Poland</i> derives from a <a href="/wiki/West_Slavs" title="West Slavs">West Slavic</a> tribe of <a href="/wiki/Polans_(western)" title="Polans (western)">Polans</a> (<i>Polanie</i>) that inhabited the <a href="/wiki/Warta_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Warta River">Warta River</a> basin of the historic <a href="/wiki/Greater_Poland" title="Greater Poland">Greater Poland</a> region in the 8th century. The origin of the name <i>Polanie</i> itself derives from the western Slavic word <i>pole</i> (field). In some foreign languages such as Hungarian, Lithuanian, Persian and Turkish the exonym for Poland is <a href="/wiki/Lechites" title="Lechites">Lechites</a> (<i>Lechici</i>), which derives from the name of a semi-legendary ruler of Polans, <a href="/wiki/Lech,_Czech,_and_Rus" title="Lech, Czech, and Rus">Lech I</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Poland" title="History of Poland">History of Poland</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory_and_protohistory">Prehistory and protohistory</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bronze-_and_Iron-Age_Poland" title="Bronze- and Iron-Age Poland">Bronze- and Iron-Age Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland_in_Antiquity" title="Poland in Antiquity">Poland in Antiquity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Poland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages" title="Poland in the Early Middle Ages">Poland in the Early Middle Ages</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Biskupin_brama_od_zewnatrz.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Biskupin_brama_od_zewnatrz.jpg/220px-Biskupin_brama_od_zewnatrz.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Biskupin_brama_od_zewnatrz.jpg/330px-Biskupin_brama_od_zewnatrz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Biskupin_brama_od_zewnatrz.jpg/440px-Biskupin_brama_od_zewnatrz.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Biskupin_brama_od_zewnatrz.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Reconstruction of a <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lusatian_culture" title="Lusatian culture">Lusatian culture</a> settlement in <a href="/wiki/Biskupin" title="Biskupin">Biskupin</a>, c. 700 BC</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Historians have postulated that throughout <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a>, many distinct ethnic groups populated the regions of what is now Poland. The ethnicity and linguistic affiliation of these groups have been hotly debated; the time and route of the original settlement of <a href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic peoples">Slavic peoples</a> in these regions lacks written records and can only be defined as fragmented.<sup id="cite_ref-muzeumczestochowa.pl_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-muzeumczestochowa.pl-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most famous archaeological find from <a href="/wiki/Prehistory_and_protohistory_of_Poland" title="Prehistory and protohistory of Poland">the prehistory and protohistory of Poland</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Biskupin" title="Biskupin">Biskupin</a> fortified settlement (now reconstructed as an open-air museum), dating from the <a href="/wiki/Lusatian_culture" title="Lusatian culture">Lusatian culture</a> of the early <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>, around 700 BC. The Slavic groups who would form Poland migrated to these areas in the second half of the 5th century AD. Up until the creation of <a href="/wiki/Mieszko_I_of_Poland" title="Mieszko I of Poland">Mieszko's</a> state and his subsequent conversion to Christianity in 966 AD, the main religion of Slavic tribes that inhabited the geographical area of present-day Poland was <a href="/wiki/Slavic_mythology" title="Slavic mythology">Slavic paganism</a>. With the <a href="/wiki/Baptism_of_Poland" class="mw-redirect" title="Baptism of Poland">Baptism of Poland</a> the Polish rulers accepted Christianity and the religious authority of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Church</a>. However, the transition from paganism was not a smooth and instantaneous process for the rest of the population as evident from the <a href="/wiki/Pagan_reaction_in_Poland" title="Pagan reaction in Poland">pagan reaction of the 1030s</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Labuda1992_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labuda1992-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Piast_dynasty">Piast dynasty</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Poland_during_the_Piast_dynasty" title="History of Poland during the Piast dynasty">History of Poland during the Piast dynasty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Civitas_Schinesghe" title="Civitas Schinesghe">Civitas Schinesghe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gesta_principum_Polonorum" title="Gesta principum Polonorum">Gesta principum Polonorum</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polska_960_-_992.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Polska_960_-_992.svg/220px-Polska_960_-_992.svg.png" width="220" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Polska_960_-_992.svg/330px-Polska_960_-_992.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Polska_960_-_992.svg/440px-Polska_960_-_992.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="11510" data-file-height="11360" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Polska_960_-_992.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of Poland under the rule of <a href="/wiki/Mieszko_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Mieszko I">Mieszko I</a> who is considered the <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> creator of the Polish state, c. 960–992</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Poland began to form into a recognizable unitary and <a href="/wiki/Territorial_entity" title="Territorial entity">territorial entity</a> around the middle of the 10th century under the <a href="/wiki/Piast_dynasty" title="Piast dynasty">Piast dynasty</a>. Poland's first <a href="/wiki/List_of_Polish_monarchs" title="List of Polish monarchs">historically documented</a> ruler, <a href="/wiki/Mieszko_I_of_Poland" title="Mieszko I of Poland">Mieszko I</a>, accepted Christianity with the <a href="/wiki/Baptism_of_Poland" class="mw-redirect" title="Baptism of Poland">Baptism of Poland</a> in 966, as the new <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">official religion</a> of his subjects. The bulk of <a href="/wiki/Baptism_of_Poland" class="mw-redirect" title="Baptism of Poland">the population converted</a> in the course of the next few centuries. In 1000, <a href="/wiki/Boles%C5%82aw_I_Chrobry" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolesław I Chrobry">Boleslaw the Brave</a>, continuing the policy of his father Mieszko, held a <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Gniezno" title="Congress of Gniezno">Congress of Gniezno</a> and created the <a href="/wiki/Metropolis_(religious_jurisdiction)" title="Metropolis (religious jurisdiction)">metropolis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gniezno" title="Gniezno">Gniezno</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Diocese" title="Diocese">dioceses</a> of <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ko%C5%82obrzeg" title="Kołobrzeg">Kołobrzeg</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wroc%C5%82aw" title="Wrocław">Wrocław</a>. However, the pagan unrest led to the transfer of the capital to Kraków in 1038 by <a href="/wiki/Casimir_I_the_Restorer" title="Casimir I the Restorer">Casimir I the Restorer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Prazmowska2011_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prazmowska2011-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Darstellung_Mieszkos_und_Mathildes_von_Schwaben.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Darstellung_Mieszkos_und_Mathildes_von_Schwaben.jpg/170px-Darstellung_Mieszkos_und_Mathildes_von_Schwaben.jpg" width="170" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Darstellung_Mieszkos_und_Mathildes_von_Schwaben.jpg/255px-Darstellung_Mieszkos_und_Mathildes_von_Schwaben.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Darstellung_Mieszkos_und_Mathildes_von_Schwaben.jpg/340px-Darstellung_Mieszkos_und_Mathildes_von_Schwaben.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Darstellung_Mieszkos_und_Mathildes_von_Schwaben.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Earliest known contemporary depiction of a Polish ruler—King <a href="/wiki/Mieszko_II_Lambert" title="Mieszko II Lambert">Mieszko II Lambert</a> of Poland, who ruled between 1025 and 1031, being presented with a <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_book" title="Liturgical book">Liturgical book</a> by <a href="/wiki/Matilda_of_Swabia" title="Matilda of Swabia">Matilda of Swabia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1109, Prince <a href="/wiki/Boles%C5%82aw_III_Wrymouth" title="Bolesław III Wrymouth">Bolesław III Wrymouth</a> defeated the King of Germany <a href="/wiki/Henry_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry V, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry V</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hundsfeld" title="Battle of Hundsfeld">Battle of Hundsfeld</a>, stopping the German march into Poland. The significance of the event was documented by <a href="/wiki/Gallus_Anonymus" title="Gallus Anonymus">Gallus Anonymus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gesta_principum_Polonorum" title="Gesta principum Polonorum">his 1118 chronicle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In 1138, Poland fragmented into several smaller duchies when Bolesław divided his lands among his sons. In 1226, <a href="/wiki/Konrad_I_of_Masovia" title="Konrad I of Masovia">Konrad I of Masovia</a>, one of the regional <a href="/wiki/Piast" class="mw-redirect" title="Piast">Piast</a> dukes, invited the <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Knights" class="mw-redirect" title="Teutonic Knights">Teutonic Knights</a> to help him fight the <a href="/wiki/Balts" title="Balts">Baltic</a> <a href="/wiki/Old_Prussians" title="Old Prussians">Prussian</a> pagans; a decision that led to centuries of warfare with the Knights. In 1264, the <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Kalisz" title="Statute of Kalisz">Statute of Kalisz</a> or the General Charter of Jewish Liberties introduced numerous right for the Jews in Poland, leading to a nearly autonomous "nation within a nation".<sup id="cite_ref-Dembkowski.2FLublin_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dembkowski.2FLublin-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the middle of the 13th century, the Silesian branch of the Piast dynasty (<a href="/wiki/Henry_I_the_Bearded" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry I the Bearded">Henry I the Bearded</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_the_Pious" title="Henry II the Pious">Henry II the Pious</a>, ruled 1238–41) nearly succeeded in uniting the Polish lands, but the <a href="/wiki/First_Mongol_invasion_of_Poland" title="First Mongol invasion of Poland">Mongols</a> invaded the country from the east and defeated the combined Polish forces at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Legnica" title="Battle of Legnica">Battle of Legnica</a> where Duke Henry II the Pious died. In 1320, after a number of earlier unsuccessful attempts by regional rulers at uniting the Polish dukedoms, <a href="/wiki/W%C5%82adys%C5%82aw_I_the_Elbow-high" title="Władysław I the Elbow-high">Władysław I</a> consolidated his power, took the throne and became the first king of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1025%E2%80%931385)" title="Kingdom of Poland (1025–1385)">a reunified Poland</a>. His son, <a href="/wiki/Casimir_III_the_Great" title="Casimir III the Great">Casimir III</a> (reigned 1333–70), has a reputation as one of the greatest Polish kings, and gained wide recognition for improving the country's infrastructure.<sup id="cite_ref-Sokol1992-60_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sokol1992-60-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Publishing2013_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Publishing2013-34">[34]</a></sup> He also extended royal protection to <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>, and encouraged their immigration to Poland.<sup id="cite_ref-Sokol1992-60_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sokol1992-60-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Haumann2002_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haumann2002-35">[35]</a></sup> Casimir III realized that the nation needed a class of educated people, especially lawyers, who could codify the country's laws and administer the courts and offices. His efforts to create an institution of higher learning in Poland were finally rewarded when <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_V" title="Pope Urban V">Pope Urban V</a> granted him permission to open the <a href="/wiki/Jagiellonian_University" title="Jagiellonian University">University of Kraków</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Casimir_the_Great_by_Leopold_L%C3%B6ffler.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Casimir_the_Great_by_Leopold_L%C3%B6ffler.PNG/170px-Casimir_the_Great_by_Leopold_L%C3%B6ffler.PNG" width="170" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Casimir_the_Great_by_Leopold_L%C3%B6ffler.PNG/255px-Casimir_the_Great_by_Leopold_L%C3%B6ffler.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Casimir_the_Great_by_Leopold_L%C3%B6ffler.PNG/340px-Casimir_the_Great_by_Leopold_L%C3%B6ffler.PNG 2x" data-file-width="470" data-file-height="734" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Casimir_the_Great_by_Leopold_L%C3%B6ffler.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Casimir_III_the_Great" title="Casimir III the Great">Casimir III the Great</a> is the only Polish king to receive the title of <i>Great</i>. He built extensively during his reign, and reformed the Polish army along with the country's civil and criminal laws, 1333–70</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Golden_Liberty" title="Golden Liberty">Golden Liberty</a> of the nobles began to develop under Casimir's rule, when in return for their <a href="/wiki/Pospolite_ruszenie" title="Pospolite ruszenie">military support</a>, the king made a series of concessions to the nobility, and establishing their legal status as superior to that of the townsmen. When Casimir the Great died in 1370, leaving no legitimate male heir, the <a href="/wiki/Piast_dynasty" title="Piast dynasty">Piast dynasty</a> came to an end.</p>
<p>During the 12th and 13th centuries, Poland became a destination for German, Flemish and to a lesser extent Scottish, Danish and Walloon migrants. Also, the Jews and Armenians began to settle and flourish in Poland during this era (see <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Poland" title="History of the Jews in Poland">History of the Jews in Poland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenians_in_Poland" title="Armenians in Poland">Armenians in Poland</a>).</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>, a plague that ravaged Europe from 1347 to 1351 did not significantly affect Poland, and the country was spared from a major outbreak of the disease.<sup id="cite_ref-REF03_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-REF03-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The reason for this was the decision of Casimir the Great to quarantine the nation's borders.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jagiellon_dynasty">Jagiellon dynasty</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Poland_during_the_Jagiellon_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Poland during the Jagiellon dynasty">History of Poland during the Jagiellon dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_in_Poland" title="Renaissance in Poland">Renaissance in Poland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Tannenberg.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Battle_of_Tannenberg.jpg/170px-Battle_of_Tannenberg.jpg" width="170" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Battle_of_Tannenberg.jpg/255px-Battle_of_Tannenberg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bb/Battle_of_Tannenberg.jpg 2x" data-file-width="307" data-file-height="312" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Tannenberg.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Grunwald" title="Battle of Grunwald">Battle of Grunwald</a> was fought against the <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Knights" class="mw-redirect" title="Teutonic Knights">German Order of Teutonic Knights</a>, and resulted in a decisive victory for the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1385%E2%80%931569)" title="Kingdom of Poland (1385–1569)">Kingdom of Poland</a>, 15 July 1410</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Jagiellon_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Jagiellon dynasty">Jagiellon dynasty</a> spanned the late <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Modern_history" title="Modern history">Modern Era</a> of Polish history. Beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duke_of_Lithuania" class="mw-redirect" title="Grand Duke of Lithuania">Lithuanian Grand Duke</a> <a href="/wiki/Jogaila" class="mw-redirect" title="Jogaila">Jogaila</a> (Władysław II Jagiełło), the Jagiellon dynasty (1386–1572) formed the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_union" title="Polish–Lithuanian union">Polish–Lithuanian union</a>. The partnership brought vast <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Lithuania</a>-controlled <a href="/wiki/Rus%27_(people)" class="mw-redirect" title="Rus' (people)">Rus' areas</a> into Poland's sphere of influence and proved beneficial for the Poles and Lithuanians, who coexisted and cooperated in one of the largest <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">political entities</a> in Europe for the next four centuries. In the Baltic Sea region Poland's struggle with the Teutonic Knights continued and culminated in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Grunwald" title="Battle of Grunwald">Battle of Grunwald</a> (1410), where a combined Polish-Lithuanian army inflicted a decisive victory against the Teutonic Knights, allowing for territorial expansion of both nations into the far north region of <a href="/wiki/Livonia" title="Livonia">Livonia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wyrozumski_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wyrozumski-38">[38]</a></sup> In 1466, after the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Years%27_War_(1454%E2%80%9366)" title="Thirteen Years' War (1454–66)">Thirteen Years' War</a>, King <a href="/wiki/Casimir_IV_Jagiellon" title="Casimir IV Jagiellon">Casimir IV Jagiellon</a> gave royal consent to the <a href="/wiki/Second_Peace_of_Thorn_(1466)" title="Second Peace of Thorn (1466)">Peace of Thorn</a>, which created the future <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Prussia" title="Duchy of Prussia">Duchy of Prussia</a>, a Polish vassal. The Jagiellon dynasty at one point also established dynastic control over the kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bohemia" title="Kingdom of Bohemia">Bohemia</a> (1471 onwards) and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hungary" title="Kingdom of Hungary">Hungary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Europe:_a_history_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europe:_a_history-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-britannica_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-40">[40]</a></sup> In the south, Poland confronted the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Khanate" title="Crimean Khanate">Crimean Tatars</a> (by whom they were attacked on 75 separate occasions between 1474 and 1569),<sup id="cite_ref-google_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google-41">[41]</a></sup> and in the east helped Lithuania fight the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Moscow" title="Grand Duchy of Moscow">Grand Duchy of Moscow</a>. Some historians estimate that Crimean Tatar slave-raiding cost Poland-Lithuania one million of its population between the years of 1494 and 1694.<sup id="cite_ref-hit-u_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hit-u-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zabudowa_Wzg%C3%B3rza_Wawelskiego_(widok_z_wie%C5%BCy_ko%C5%9Bcio%C5%82a_Mariackiego);_A-7;_PL-MA,_Krak%C3%B3w,_Wawel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Zabudowa_Wzg%C3%B3rza_Wawelskiego_%28widok_z_wie%C5%BCy_ko%C5%9Bcio%C5%82a_Mariackiego%29%3B_A-7%3B_PL-MA%2C_Krak%C3%B3w%2C_Wawel.jpg/400px-Zabudowa_Wzg%C3%B3rza_Wawelskiego_%28widok_z_wie%C5%BCy_ko%C5%9Bcio%C5%82a_Mariackiego%29%3B_A-7%3B_PL-MA%2C_Krak%C3%B3w%2C_Wawel.jpg" width="400" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Zabudowa_Wzg%C3%B3rza_Wawelskiego_%28widok_z_wie%C5%BCy_ko%C5%9Bcio%C5%82a_Mariackiego%29%3B_A-7%3B_PL-MA%2C_Krak%C3%B3w%2C_Wawel.jpg/600px-Zabudowa_Wzg%C3%B3rza_Wawelskiego_%28widok_z_wie%C5%BCy_ko%C5%9Bcio%C5%82a_Mariackiego%29%3B_A-7%3B_PL-MA%2C_Krak%C3%B3w%2C_Wawel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Zabudowa_Wzg%C3%B3rza_Wawelskiego_%28widok_z_wie%C5%BCy_ko%C5%9Bcio%C5%82a_Mariackiego%29%3B_A-7%3B_PL-MA%2C_Krak%C3%B3w%2C_Wawel.jpg/800px-Zabudowa_Wzg%C3%B3rza_Wawelskiego_%28widok_z_wie%C5%BCy_ko%C5%9Bcio%C5%82a_Mariackiego%29%3B_A-7%3B_PL-MA%2C_Krak%C3%B3w%2C_Wawel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2858" data-file-height="953" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zabudowa_Wzg%C3%B3rza_Wawelskiego_(widok_z_wie%C5%BCy_ko%C5%9Bcio%C5%82a_Mariackiego);_A-7;_PL-MA,_Krak%C3%B3w,_Wawel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Wawel_Castle" title="Wawel Castle">Wawel Castle</a> in <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a>, seat of Polish kings from 1038 until the capital was moved to <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a> in 1596. The royal residence is an example of early <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_in_Poland" title="Renaissance in Poland">Renaissance</a> architecture in Poland</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Poland was developing as a <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudal</a> state, with a predominantly agricultural economy and an increasingly powerful <a href="/wiki/Landed_nobility" title="Landed nobility">landed nobility</a>. The <i><a href="/wiki/Nihil_novi" title="Nihil novi">Nihil novi</a></i> act adopted by the Polish <a href="/wiki/General_sejm" class="mw-redirect" title="General sejm">Sejm</a> (parliament) in 1505, transferred most of the <a href="/wiki/Legislature" title="Legislature">legislative power</a> from the monarch to the Sejm, an event which marked the beginning of the period known as "Golden Liberty", when the state was ruled by the "free and equal" <a href="/wiki/Szlachta" title="Szlachta">Polish nobility</a>. <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> movements made deep inroads into Polish Christianity, which resulted in the establishment of policies promoting religious tolerance, unique in Europe at that time.<sup id="cite_ref-LouthanCohen2011_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LouthanCohen2011-43">[43]</a></sup> This tolerance allowed the country to avoid most the religious turmoil that spread over Europe during the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-LouthanCohen2011_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LouthanCohen2011-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The European <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> evoked in late Jagiellon Poland (kings <a href="/wiki/Sigismund_I_the_Old" title="Sigismund I the Old">Sigismund I the Old</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sigismund_II_Augustus" title="Sigismund II Augustus">Sigismund II Augustus</a>) a sense of urgency in the need to promote a <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_in_Poland" title="Renaissance in Poland">cultural awakening</a>, and during this period Polish culture and the nation's economy flourished. In 1543, <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a> a Polish astronomer from <a href="/wiki/Toru%C5%84" title="Toruń">Toruń</a>, published his epochal work <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">De revolutionibus orbium coelestium</a></i> (<i>On the Revolutions of the Celestial Spheres</i>), and thereby became the first proponent of a predictive mathematical model confirming the <a href="/wiki/Copernican_heliocentrism" title="Copernican heliocentrism">heliocentric theory</a>, which became the accepted basic model for the practice of modern astronomy. Another major figure associated with the era is the classicist poet <a href="/wiki/Jan_Kochanowski" title="Jan Kochanowski">Jan Kochanowski</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gierowski_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gierowski-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polish.E2.80.93Lithuanian_Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Poland_in_the_Early_Modern_era_(1569%E2%80%931795)" title="History of Poland in the Early Modern era (1569–1795)">History of Poland in the Early Modern era (1569–1795)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sarmatism" title="Sarmatism">Sarmatism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Konfederacja_Warszawska.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Konfederacja_Warszawska.jpg/220px-Konfederacja_Warszawska.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Konfederacja_Warszawska.jpg/330px-Konfederacja_Warszawska.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Konfederacja_Warszawska.jpg/440px-Konfederacja_Warszawska.jpg 2x" data-file-width="11393" data-file-height="7772" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Konfederacja_Warszawska.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Confederation" title="Warsaw Confederation">Warsaw Confederation</a> was an important development in the history of Poland that extended religious freedoms and tolerance, and is the first such document in Europe, 28 January 1573</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 1569 <a href="/wiki/Union_of_Lublin" title="Union of Lublin">Union of Lublin</a> established the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a>, a more closely unified federal state with an <a href="/wiki/Elective_monarchy" title="Elective monarchy">elective monarchy</a>, but which was governed largely by the nobility, through a system of <a href="/wiki/Sejmik" title="Sejmik">local assemblies</a> with a central parliament. The <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Confederation" title="Warsaw Confederation">Warsaw Confederation</a> (1573) confirmed the religious freedom of all residents of Poland, which was extremely important for the stability of the multiethnic Polish society of the time.<sup id="cite_ref-Dembkowski.2FLublin_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dembkowski.2FLublin-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">Serfdom</a> was banned in 1588.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The establishment of the Commonwealth coincided with a period of stability and prosperity in Poland, with the union thereafter becoming a European power and a major cultural entity, occupying approximately one million square kilometers of Central and Eastern Europe, as well as an agent for the dissemination of <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a> through <a href="/wiki/Polonization" title="Polonization">Polonization</a> into areas of modern-day Lithuania, Ukraine, Belarus and Western Russia.</p>
<p>In the 16th and 17th centuries, <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Poland</a> suffered from a number of dynastic crises during the reigns of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Vasa" title="House of Vasa">Vasa</a> kings <a href="/wiki/Sigismund_III_Vasa" title="Sigismund III Vasa">Sigismund III</a> and <a href="/wiki/W%C5%82adys%C5%82aw_IV_Vasa" title="Władysław IV Vasa">Władysław IV</a> and found itself engaged in major conflicts with <a href="/wiki/Tsardom_of_Russia" title="Tsardom of Russia">Russia</a>, Sweden and the Ottoman Empire, as well as a series of minor <a href="/wiki/Cossacks" title="Cossacks">Cossack</a> uprisings.<sup id="cite_ref-gierowski_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gierowski-46">[46]</a></sup> In 1610 <a href="/wiki/Hetman" title="Hetman">Hetman</a> <a href="/wiki/Stanis%C5%82aw_%C5%BB%C3%B3%C5%82kiewski" title="Stanisław Żółkiewski">Stanisław Żółkiewski</a> seized Moscow after winning the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Klushino" title="Battle of Klushino">Battle of Klushino</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polish-Lithuanian_Commonwealth_in_1635.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Polish-Lithuanian_Commonwealth_in_1635.svg/300px-Polish-Lithuanian_Commonwealth_in_1635.svg.png" width="300" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Polish-Lithuanian_Commonwealth_in_1635.svg/450px-Polish-Lithuanian_Commonwealth_in_1635.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Polish-Lithuanian_Commonwealth_in_1635.svg/600px-Polish-Lithuanian_Commonwealth_in_1635.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1411" data-file-height="1106" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Polish-Lithuanian_Commonwealth_in_1635.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a> at its greatest extent after the <a href="/wiki/Truce_of_Deulino" title="Truce of Deulino">Truce of Deulino</a>. In the years 1618–1621 Poland covered an area of 990 000 km²</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From the middle of the 17th century, the nobles' democracy, suffering from internal disorder, gradually declined, thereby leaving the once powerful Commonwealth vulnerable to foreign intervention. Starting in 1648, the <a href="/wiki/Cossack" class="mw-redirect" title="Cossack">Cossack</a> <a href="/wiki/Khmelnytsky_Uprising" title="Khmelnytsky Uprising">Khmelnytsky Uprising</a> engulfed the south and east, eventually leaving Ukraine divided, with the eastern part, lost by the Commonwealth, becoming a dependency of the Tsardom of Russia. This was followed by the <a href="/wiki/Deluge_(history)" title="Deluge (history)">'Deluge'</a>, a Swedish invasion of Poland, which marched through the Polish heartlands and ruined the country's population, culture and infrastructure. Around four million of Poland's eleven million inhabitants died in famines and epidemics.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica1_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica1-47">[47]</a></sup> However, under <a href="/wiki/John_III_Sobieski" title="John III Sobieski">John III Sobieski</a> the Commonwealth's military prowess was re-established, and in 1683 Polish forces played a major role in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Vienna" title="Battle of Vienna">Battle of Vienna</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Army_(15th-19th_centuries)" title="Ottoman Army (15th-19th centuries)">Ottoman Army</a>, commanded by <a href="/wiki/Kara_Mustafa_Pasha" title="Kara Mustafa Pasha">Kara Mustafa</a> the grand vizier of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Schultz_John_III_Sobieski.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Schultz_John_III_Sobieski.jpg/170px-Schultz_John_III_Sobieski.jpg" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Schultz_John_III_Sobieski.jpg/255px-Schultz_John_III_Sobieski.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Schultz_John_III_Sobieski.jpg/340px-Schultz_John_III_Sobieski.jpg 2x" data-file-width="564" data-file-height="758" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Schultz_John_III_Sobieski.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
King <a href="/wiki/John_III_Sobieski" title="John III Sobieski">John III Sobieski</a> defeated the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Turks</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Vienna" title="Battle of Vienna">Battle of Vienna</a> on 12 September 1683</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sobieski's reign marked the end of the nation's golden-era. Finding itself subjected to almost constant warfare and suffering enormous population losses as well as massive damage to its economy, the Commonwealth fell into decline. The government became ineffective as a result of large-scale internal conflicts (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Lubomirski_Rebellion" class="mw-redirect" title="Lubomirski Rebellion">Lubomirski Rebellion</a> against <a href="/wiki/John_II_Casimir_Vasa" title="John II Casimir Vasa">John II Casimir</a> and rebellious <a href="/wiki/Confederation_(Poland)" title="Confederation (Poland)">confederations</a>) and corrupted legislative processes. The nobility fell under the control of a handful of <i><a href="/wiki/Magnat_(Poland)" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnat (Poland)">magnats</a></i>, and this, compounded with two relatively weak kings of the <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_Saxony" title="Electorate of Saxony">Saxon</a> <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wettin" title="House of Wettin">Wettin dynasty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Augustus_II_the_Strong" title="Augustus II the Strong">Augustus II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustus_III_of_Poland" title="Augustus III of Poland">Augustus III</a>, as well as the rise of <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Great_Northern_War" title="Great Northern War">Great Northern War</a> only served to worsen the Commonwealth's plight. Despite this The Commonwealth-Saxony <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> gave rise to the emergence of the Commonwealth's first reform movement, and laid the foundations for the <a href="/wiki/Enlightenment_in_Poland" title="Enlightenment in Poland">Polish Enlightenment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gierowski2_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gierowski2-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stanis%C5%82aw_II_August_Poniatowski_in_coronation_clothes.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Stanis%C5%82aw_II_August_Poniatowski_in_coronation_clothes.PNG/170px-Stanis%C5%82aw_II_August_Poniatowski_in_coronation_clothes.PNG" width="170" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Stanis%C5%82aw_II_August_Poniatowski_in_coronation_clothes.PNG/255px-Stanis%C5%82aw_II_August_Poniatowski_in_coronation_clothes.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Stanis%C5%82aw_II_August_Poniatowski_in_coronation_clothes.PNG/340px-Stanis%C5%82aw_II_August_Poniatowski_in_coronation_clothes.PNG 2x" data-file-width="1755" data-file-height="2510" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stanis%C5%82aw_II_August_Poniatowski_in_coronation_clothes.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Stanis%C5%82aw_II_August" class="mw-redirect" title="Stanisław II August">Stanisław II August</a>, the last King of Poland—acceded to the throne in 1764 and reigned until his abdication on 25 November 1795</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the later part of the 18th century, the Commonwealth made attempts to implement fundamental internal reforms; with the second half of the century bringing a much improved economy, significant population growth and far-reaching progress in the areas of education, intellectual life, art, and especially toward the end of the period, evolution of the social and political system. The most populous capital city of <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a> replaced <a href="/wiki/Gda%C5%84sk" title="Gdańsk">Gdańsk</a> (Danzig) as the leading centre of commerce, and the role of the more prosperous townsmen increased.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_of_partitions">Age of partitions</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Poland_(1795%E2%80%931918)" title="History of Poland (1795–1918)">History of Poland (1795–1918)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Partitions_of_Poland" title="Partitions of Poland">Partitions of Poland</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Royal_elections_in_Poland" title="Royal elections in Poland">royal election</a> of 1764 resulted in the elevation of <a href="/wiki/Stanis%C5%82aw_August_Poniatowski" title="Stanisław August Poniatowski">Stanisław II August</a> (a Polish aristocrat connected to the <a href="/wiki/Czartoryski_family" class="mw-redirect" title="Czartoryski family">Czartoryski family</a> faction of <a href="/wiki/Magnates" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnates">magnates</a>) to the monarchy. However, as a one-time personal admirer of Empress <a href="/wiki/Catherine_II_of_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Catherine II of Russia">Catherine II of Russia</a>, the new king spent much of his reign torn between his desire to implement reforms necessary to save his nation, and his perceived necessity to remain in a political relationship with his Russian sponsor. This led to the formation of the 1768 <a href="/wiki/Bar_Confederation" title="Bar Confederation">Bar Confederation</a>, a <i>szlachta</i> rebellion directed against the Polish king and his Russian sponsors, which aimed to preserve Poland's independence and the szlachta's traditional privileges. Attempts at reform provoked the union's neighbours, and in 1772 the <a href="/wiki/First_Partition_of_Poland" title="First Partition of Poland">First Partition of the Commonwealth</a> by Prussia, Russia and Austria took place; an act which the "<a href="/wiki/Partition_Sejm" title="Partition Sejm">Partition Sejm</a>", under considerable duress, eventually "ratified" <i>fait accompli</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-wydawnictwo_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wydawnictwo-49">[49]</a></sup> Disregarding this loss, in 1773 the king established the <a href="/wiki/Commission_of_National_Education" title="Commission of National Education">Commission of National Education</a>, the first government education authority in Europe. Corporal punishment of children was officially prohibited in 1783.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oath_of_confirmation_of_Constitution_of_the_3rd_May_1791.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Oath_of_confirmation_of_Constitution_of_the_3rd_May_1791.PNG/220px-Oath_of_confirmation_of_Constitution_of_the_3rd_May_1791.PNG" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Oath_of_confirmation_of_Constitution_of_the_3rd_May_1791.PNG/330px-Oath_of_confirmation_of_Constitution_of_the_3rd_May_1791.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Oath_of_confirmation_of_Constitution_of_the_3rd_May_1791.PNG/440px-Oath_of_confirmation_of_Constitution_of_the_3rd_May_1791.PNG 2x" data-file-width="1538" data-file-height="1083" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oath_of_confirmation_of_Constitution_of_the_3rd_May_1791.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_May_3,_1791" title="Constitution of May 3, 1791"><i>Constitution of May 3rd</i></a>, enactment ceremony inside the Senate Chamber at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Castle,_Warsaw" title="Royal Castle, Warsaw">Warsaw Royal Castle</a>, 1791</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Sejm" title="Great Sejm">Great Sejm</a> convened by Stanisław II August in 1788 successfully adopted the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_May_3,_1791" title="Constitution of May 3, 1791">3 May Constitution</a>, the first set of modern supreme national laws in Europe. However, this document, accused by detractors of harbouring revolutionary sympathies, generated strong opposition from the Commonwealth's nobles and conservatives as well as from Catherine II, who, determined to prevent the rebirth of a strong Commonwealth set about planning the final dismemberment of the Polish-Lithuanian state. Russia was aided in achieving its goal when the <a href="/wiki/Targowica_Confederation" title="Targowica Confederation">Targowica Confederation</a>, an organisation of Polish nobles, appealed to the Empress for help. In May 1792, Russian forces crossed the Commonwealth's frontier, thus beginning the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Russian_War_of_1792" title="Polish–Russian War of 1792">Polish-Russian War</a>.</p>
<p>The defensive war fought by the Poles ended prematurely when the King, convinced of the futility of resistance, capitulated and joined the Targowica Confederation. The Confederation then took over the government. Russia and Prussia, fearing the mere existence of a Polish state, arranged for, and in 1793 executed, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Partition_of_Poland" title="Second Partition of Poland">Second Partition of the Commonwealth</a>, which left the country deprived of so much territory that it was practically incapable of independent existence. Eventually, in 1795, following the failed <a href="/wiki/Ko%C5%9Bciuszko_Uprising" title="Kościuszko Uprising">Kościuszko Uprising</a>, the Commonwealth was <a href="/wiki/Third_Partition_of_Poland" title="Third Partition of Poland">partitioned one last time</a> by all three of its more powerful neighbours, and with this, effectively ceased to exist.<sup id="cite_ref-gierowski3_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gierowski3-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Era_of_insurrections">Era of insurrections</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Warsaw" title="Duchy of Warsaw">Duchy of Warsaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Posen" title="Grand Duchy of Posen">Grand Duchy of Posen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Galicia_and_Lodomeria" title="Kingdom of Galicia and Lodomeria">Kingdom of Galicia and Lodomeria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Congress_Poland" title="Congress Poland">Congress Poland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rzeczpospolita_Rozbiory_3.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Rzeczpospolita_Rozbiory_3.png/300px-Rzeczpospolita_Rozbiory_3.png" width="300" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Rzeczpospolita_Rozbiory_3.png/450px-Rzeczpospolita_Rozbiory_3.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Rzeczpospolita_Rozbiory_3.png/600px-Rzeczpospolita_Rozbiory_3.png 2x" data-file-width="1906" data-file-height="1568" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rzeczpospolita_Rozbiory_3.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Partitions_of_Poland" title="Partitions of Poland">Partitions of Poland</a>, carried out by <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia" title="Kingdom of Prussia">Prussia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Monarchy" title="Habsburg Monarchy">Austria</a> in <a href="/wiki/First_Partition_of_Poland" title="First Partition of Poland">1772</a>, <a href="/wiki/Second_Partition_of_Poland" title="Second Partition of Poland">1793</a> and <a href="/wiki/Third_Partition_of_Poland" title="Third Partition of Poland">1795</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Poles <a href="/wiki/List_of_wars_involving_Poland" title="List of wars involving Poland">rebelled several times against the partitioners</a>, particularly near the end of the 18th century and the beginning of the 19th century. An unsuccessful attempt at defending Poland's sovereignty took place in 1794 during the <a href="/wiki/Ko%C5%9Bciuszko_Uprising" title="Kościuszko Uprising">Kościuszko Uprising</a>, where a popular and distinguished general <a href="/wiki/Tadeusz_Kosciuszko" class="mw-redirect" title="Tadeusz Kosciuszko">Tadeusz Kosciuszko</a>, who had several years earlier served under <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">Washington</a> in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>, led Polish insurrectionists against numerically superior Russian forces. Despite the victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Rac%C5%82awice" title="Battle of Racławice">Battle of Racławice</a>, his ultimate defeat ended Poland's independent existence <a href="/wiki/Third_Partition_of_Poland" title="Third Partition of Poland">for 123 years</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gardner_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gardner-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stachowicz_Przysi%C4%99ga_T._Ko%C5%9Bciuszki_na_rynku_w_Krakowie.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Stachowicz_Przysi%C4%99ga_T._Ko%C5%9Bciuszki_na_rynku_w_Krakowie.jpg/220px-Stachowicz_Przysi%C4%99ga_T._Ko%C5%9Bciuszki_na_rynku_w_Krakowie.jpg" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Stachowicz_Przysi%C4%99ga_T._Ko%C5%9Bciuszki_na_rynku_w_Krakowie.jpg/330px-Stachowicz_Przysi%C4%99ga_T._Ko%C5%9Bciuszki_na_rynku_w_Krakowie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Stachowicz_Przysi%C4%99ga_T._Ko%C5%9Bciuszki_na_rynku_w_Krakowie.jpg/440px-Stachowicz_Przysi%C4%99ga_T._Ko%C5%9Bciuszki_na_rynku_w_Krakowie.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="506" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stachowicz_Przysi%C4%99ga_T._Ko%C5%9Bciuszki_na_rynku_w_Krakowie.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Tadeusz_Ko%C5%9Bciuszko" title="Tadeusz Kościuszko">Tadeusz Kościuszko</a> takes the <a href="/wiki/Ko%C5%9Bciuszko%27s_proclamation" title="Kościuszko's proclamation">oath of loyalty to the Polish nation</a> in <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a>, vowing to fight against <a href="/wiki/Second_Partition_of_Poland" title="Second Partition of Poland">military interventions</a> of the partitioning powers, 1794</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1807, <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon I of France</a> temporarily recreated a Polish state as the satellite <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Warsaw" title="Duchy of Warsaw">Duchy of Warsaw</a>, after a successful <a href="/wiki/Greater_Poland_Uprising_(1806)" title="Greater Poland Uprising (1806)">Greater Poland Uprising of 1806</a> against Prussian rule. But, after the failed <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, Poland was again split between the victorious powers at the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a> of 1815.<sup id="cite_ref-Lukowski313_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lukowski313-52">[52]</a></sup> The eastern part was ruled by the Russian <a href="/wiki/Tsar" title="Tsar">tsar</a> as <a href="/wiki/Congress_Poland" title="Congress Poland">Congress Poland</a>, which had a very <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Kingdom_of_Poland" title="Constitution of the Kingdom of Poland">liberal constitution</a>. However, over time the Russian tsar reduced Polish freedoms, and Russia annexed the country in virtually all but name. Meanwhile, the Prussian controlled territory of Poland came under increased Germanization. Thus, in the 19th century, only Austrian ruled <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Central_Europe)" class="mw-redirect" title="Galicia (Central Europe)">Galicia</a>, and particularly the <a href="/wiki/Free_City_of_Krak%C3%B3w" title="Free City of Kraków">Free City of Kraków</a>, allowed free Polish culture to flourish.</p>
<p>Throughout the period of the partitions, political and cultural repression of the Polish nation led to the organisation of a number of uprisings against the authorities of the occupying Russian, Prussian and Austrian governments.</p>
<p>In 1830, the <a href="/wiki/November_Uprising" title="November Uprising">November Uprising</a> began in <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a> when, led by Lieutenant <a href="/wiki/Piotr_Wysocki" title="Piotr Wysocki">Piotr Wysocki</a>, young <a href="/wiki/Non-commissioned_officer" title="Non-commissioned officer">non-commissioned officers</a> at the <a href="https://pl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Szko%C5%82a_Podchor%C4%85%C5%BCych_w_Warszawie" class="extiw" title="pl:Szkoła Podchorążych w Warszawie">Officer Cadet School</a> in Warsaw revolted. They were joined by large segments of Polish society, and together forced Warsaw's Russian garrison to withdraw north of the city.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MWP_1830_Arsenal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/MWP_1830_Arsenal.jpg/220px-MWP_1830_Arsenal.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/MWP_1830_Arsenal.jpg/330px-MWP_1830_Arsenal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/MWP_1830_Arsenal.jpg/440px-MWP_1830_Arsenal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1860" data-file-height="1240" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MWP_1830_Arsenal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Capture of the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Arsenal" title="Warsaw Arsenal">Warsaw Arsenal</a> by the Polish army during the <a href="/wiki/November_Uprising" title="November Uprising">November Uprising</a> against the rule of the Russian <a href="/wiki/Tsar" title="Tsar">Tsar</a>, 29 November 1830</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Over the course of the next seven months, Polish forces successfully defeated the Russian armies of Field Marshal <a href="/wiki/Hans_Karl_von_Diebitsch" title="Hans Karl von Diebitsch">Hans Karl von Diebitsch</a> and a number of other Russian commanders; however, finding themselves in a position unsupported by any other foreign powers, save distant France and the newborn United States, and with Prussia and Austria refusing to allow the import of military supplies through their territories, the Poles accepted that the uprising was doomed to failure. Upon the surrender of Warsaw to General <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Paskievich" class="mw-redirect" title="Ivan Paskievich">Ivan Paskievich</a>, many Polish troops, feeling they could not go on, withdrew into Prussia and there laid down their arms. After the defeat, the semi-independent Congress Poland lost its constitution, army and legislative assembly, and was integrated more closely with the Russian Empire.</p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Spring_of_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Spring of Nations">Spring of Nations</a> (a series of revolutions which swept across Europe), Poles took up arms in the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Poland_Uprising_(1848)" title="Greater Poland Uprising (1848)">Greater Poland Uprising of 1848</a> to resist Prussian rule. Initially, the uprising manifested itself in the form of civil disobedience, but eventually turned into an armed struggle when the Prussian military was sent in to pacify the region. Eventually, after several battles the uprising was suppressed by the Prussians, and the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Posen" title="Grand Duchy of Posen">Grand Duchy of Posen</a> was stripped of its autonomy and completely incorporated into the <a href="/wiki/German_Confederation" title="German Confederation">German Confederation</a>.</p>
<p>In 1863, a new Polish uprising against Russian rule began. The <a href="/wiki/January_Uprising" title="January Uprising">January Uprising</a> started out as a spontaneous protest by young Poles against <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscription</a> into the Imperial Russian Army. However, the insurrectionists, despite being joined by high-ranking Polish-Lithuanian officers and numerous politicians, were still severely outnumbered and lacking in foreign support. They were forced to resort to guerrilla warfare tactics and failed to win any major military victories. Afterwards no major uprising was witnessed in the Russian-controlled Congress Poland, and Poles resorted instead to fostering economic and cultural self-improvement.</p>
<p>Despite the political unrest experienced during the partitions, Poland did benefit from large-scale industrialisation and modernisation programs, instituted by the occupying powers, which helped it develop into a more economically coherent and viable entity. This was particularly true in Greater Poland, Silesia and Eastern Pomerania controlled by Prussia (later becoming a part of the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a>); areas which eventually, thanks largely to the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Poland_Uprising_(1918%E2%80%931919)" title="Greater Poland Uprising (1918–1919)">Greater Poland Uprising of 1918</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silesian_Uprisings" title="Silesian Uprisings">Silesian Uprisings</a>, were reconstituted as a part of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Polish_Republic" title="Second Polish Republic">Second Polish Republic</a>, becoming the country's most prosperous regions.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reconstruction">Reconstruction</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Poland_(1918%E2%80%9339)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Poland (1918–39)">History of Poland (1918–39)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1916%E2%80%9318)" title="Kingdom of Poland (1916–18)">Kingdom of Poland (1916–18)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Second_Polish_Republic" title="Second Polish Republic">Second Polish Republic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Warsaw_(1920)" title="Battle of Warsaw (1920)">Battle of Warsaw (1920)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jozef_Pilsudski.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Jozef_Pilsudski.jpg/170px-Jozef_Pilsudski.jpg" width="170" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Jozef_Pilsudski.jpg/255px-Jozef_Pilsudski.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Jozef_Pilsudski.jpg/340px-Jozef_Pilsudski.jpg 2x" data-file-width="416" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jozef_Pilsudski.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Naczelnik_pa%C5%84stwa" class="mw-redirect" title="Naczelnik państwa">Chief of State</a> Marshal <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zef_Pi%C5%82sudski" title="Józef Piłsudski">Józef Piłsudski</a> was the nation's premiere statesman between 1918 until his death on 12 May 1935</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, all the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allies</a> agreed on the reconstitution of Poland that United States President <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a> proclaimed in Point 13 of his <a href="/wiki/Fourteen_Points" title="Fourteen Points">Fourteen Points</a>. A total of 2 million Polish troops fought with the armies of the three occupying powers, and 450,000 died. Shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Armistice_with_Germany_(Compi%C3%A8gne)" class="mw-redirect" title="Armistice with Germany (Compiègne)">armistice with Germany in November 1918</a>, Poland regained its independence as the <a href="/wiki/Second_Polish_Republic" title="Second Polish Republic">Second Polish Republic</a> (<i>II Rzeczpospolita Polska</i>). It reaffirmed its independence after <a href="/wiki/List_of_wars_involving_Poland" title="List of wars involving Poland">a series of military conflicts</a>, the most notable being the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Soviet_War" title="Polish–Soviet War">Polish–Soviet War</a> (1919–21) when Poland inflicted a crushing defeat on the <a href="/wiki/Red_Army" title="Red Army">Red Army</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Warsaw_(1920)" title="Battle of Warsaw (1920)">Battle of Warsaw</a>, an event which is considered to have halted the advance of Communism into Europe and forced <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a> to rethink his objective of achieving global socialism. The event is often referred to as the "Miracle at the Vistula".<sup id="cite_ref-G.C5.82os_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G.C5.82os-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RzeczpospolitaII.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/RzeczpospolitaII.png/220px-RzeczpospolitaII.png" width="220" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/RzeczpospolitaII.png/330px-RzeczpospolitaII.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/RzeczpospolitaII.png/440px-RzeczpospolitaII.png 2x" data-file-width="1112" data-file-height="1069" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RzeczpospolitaII.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of Poland during the <a href="/wiki/Interwar_period" title="Interwar period">Interwar period</a>, 1918–39</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During this period, Poland successfully managed to fuse the territories of the three former partitioning powers into a cohesive nation state. Railways were restructured to direct traffic towards <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a> instead of the former imperial capitals, a new network of national roads was gradually built up and a <a href="/wiki/Gdynia" title="Gdynia">major seaport</a> was opened on the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic</a> Coast, so as to allow Polish exports and imports to bypass the politically charged <a href="/wiki/Free_City_of_Danzig" title="Free City of Danzig">Free City of Danzig</a>.</p>
<p>The inter-war period heralded in a new era of Polish politics. Whilst Polish political activists had faced heavy censorship in the decades up until the First World War, the country now found itself trying to establish a new political tradition. For this reason, many exiled Polish activists, such as <a href="/wiki/Ignacy_Jan_Paderewski" title="Ignacy Jan Paderewski">Ignacy Paderewski</a> (who would later become prime minister) returned home to help; a significant number of them then went on to take key positions in the newly formed political and governmental structures. Tragedy struck in 1922 when <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Narutowicz" title="Gabriel Narutowicz">Gabriel Narutowicz</a>, inaugural holder of the presidency, was assassinated at the <a href="/wiki/Zach%C4%99ta" title="Zachęta">Zachęta Gallery</a> in Warsaw by painter and right-wing nationalist <a href="/wiki/Eligiusz_Niewiadomski" title="Eligiusz Niewiadomski">Eligiusz Niewiadomski</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bitter_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bitter-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 1926 <a href="/wiki/May_Coup_(Poland)" title="May Coup (Poland)">May Coup</a> of <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zef_Pi%C5%82sudski" title="Józef Piłsudski">Józef Piłsudski</a> turned rule of the Second Polish Republic over to the <a href="/wiki/Sanacja" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanacja">Sanacja</a> movement. By the 1930s Poland had become increasingly authoritarian; a number of 'undesirable' political parties, such as the Polish Communists, had been banned and following Piłsudski's death, the regime, unable to appoint a new leader, began to show its inherent internal weaknesses and unwillingness to cooperate in any way with other political parties.</p>
<p>As result of the <a href="/wiki/Munich_Agreement" title="Munich Agreement">Munich Agreement</a> in 1938, major European powers (Germany, France, Britain and Italy) awarded Poland the small 350 sq mi <a href="/wiki/Zaolzie" title="Zaolzie">Zaolzie Region</a> of Czechoslovakia. The area was a point of contention between the Polish and Czechoslovak governments in the past and the two countries <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Czechoslovak_War" title="Polish–Czechoslovak War">fought a brief seven-day war over it in 1919</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Poland_(1939%E2%80%9345)" title="History of Poland (1939–45)">History of Poland (1939–45)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland" title="Invasion of Poland">Invasion of Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polish_contribution_to_World_War_II" title="Polish contribution to World War II">Polish contribution to World War II</a>, and <a href="/wiki/War_crimes_in_occupied_Poland_during_World_War_II" title="War crimes in occupied Poland during World War II">War crimes in occupied Poland during World War II</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:7TP-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/7TP-2.jpg/170px-7TP-2.jpg" width="170" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/7TP-2.jpg/255px-7TP-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/7TP-2.jpg/340px-7TP-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="650" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:7TP-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Polish army's <a href="/wiki/7TP" title="7TP">7TP</a> tanks during military maneuvers shortly before the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland" title="Invasion of Poland">Invasion of Poland</a>, 1939</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The formal beginning of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> was marked by the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_German" class="mw-redirect" title="Nazi German">Nazi German</a> <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland_(1939)" class="mw-redirect" title="Invasion of Poland (1939)">invasion of Poland</a> on 1 September 1939, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_invasion_of_Poland" title="Soviet invasion of Poland">Soviet invasion of Poland</a> on 17 September in violation of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Polish_Non-Aggression_Pact" title="Soviet–Polish Non-Aggression Pact">Soviet–Polish Non-Aggression Pact</a>. On 28 September 1939 <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Warsaw_(1939)" title="Siege of Warsaw (1939)">Warsaw capitulated</a>. As agreed earlier in the <a href="/wiki/Molotov%E2%80%93Ribbentrop_Pact" title="Molotov–Ribbentrop Pact">Molotov–Ribbentrop Pact</a>, Poland was split into two occupied zones, <a href="/wiki/Polish_areas_annexed_by_Nazi_Germany" title="Polish areas annexed by Nazi Germany">one subdivided by Nazi Germany</a>, while the other, including all of eastern <a href="/wiki/Kresy" title="Kresy">Kresy</a> fell under <a href="/wiki/Territories_of_Poland_annexed_by_the_Soviet_Union" title="Territories of Poland annexed by the Soviet Union">the control of the Soviet Union</a>. In 1939–41, the Soviets had deported hundreds of thousands of Poles out to the most distant parts of the Soviet Union. The Soviet <a href="/wiki/NKVD" title="NKVD">NKVD</a> secretly executed thousands of Polish prisoners of war (inter alia <a href="/wiki/Katyn_massacre" title="Katyn massacre">Katyn massacre</a>) ahead of the <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">Operation Barbarossa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-55">[55]</a></sup> German planners had in November 1939 called for "the complete destruction" of all Poles and their fate, as well as many other <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavs</a>, was outlined in genocidal <i><a href="/wiki/Generalplan_Ost" title="Generalplan Ost">Generalplan Ost</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
